# Chapter 1

## **CHAPTER 1**

Caius’s sandals slapped the same Roman cobbles he’d walked as a boy. The market stank of fish and garum, the sun hammered his neck, and the laughter from a wineshop was a dialect he no longer spoke. Home was a city that remembered a different man.


He’d been here seventeen days. Long enough for the coin in his purse to grow thin and anxious, long enough to learn that the wine was vinegar cut with honey and the bread had grit in it, long enough for the ache in his left shoulder—the old legion wound—to become a constant, damp-throated companion. He was fifty-two. An age for a man to be sitting in his own *domus*, watching his grandchildren chase lizards in the courtyard, not standing in a barbarian drizzle waiting for a man who sold nails.

“Strabo?”

The voice was Brittonic, the Latin shaped around a thicker, softer tongue. Lucius didn’t turn immediately. He finished his survey of a flat-bottomed barge being poled upstream, its pilot a hulking silhouette in a hood of hide. Then he inclined his head. “Vellocatus.”

The man was short, built like a barrel, with forearms matted with black hair and a face that seemed permanently squinted against weather or suspicion. He wore a tunic of coarse, undyed wool and breeches. A Roman in Rome would have laughed at him. Here, he had a dry storeroom, a forge, and a reputation for metal that didn’t snap in the cold.

“You have them?” Lucius asked.

Vellocatus grunted, hefting a leather sack. It clanked dully. “Twenty, as asked. Good iron. Not that soft shit from the east.” He peered at Lucius. “You’re building?”

“Repairing,” Lucius said. He took the sack, the weight solid and reassuring. He counted out the sesterces into the Briton’s calloused palm. The transaction was silent, efficient. There was no gossip, no inquiry about family, no offer of a cup of wine. It was what Lucius preferred. In Rome, every exchange was a negotiation layered with politics, favours owed, subtle insults. Here, it was just the thing, and the coin. There was a brutal honesty to it he could respect, even as it chilled him.

“That’s it, then,” Vellocatus said, pocketing the coins. He hesitated, his eyes flicking over Lucius’s attire—the worn but well-made *paenula*, the boots that had seen Italian sun and Danubian mud. “You’re the one they talk about. The senator’s man.”

Lucius’s gaze sharpened. “I am no one’s man.”

A shrug. “Word is you served old Senator Gallus. Word is he fell foul of the wrong people. Word is you didn’t fall with him. Makes some wonder.”

“Let them wonder in the rain,” Lucius said, his voice flat. He slung the sack of nails over his good shoulder. “My business is with wood and mortar, not words.”

He left the Briton standing there and stepped back into the drizzle. The path was a slurry of gravel and mud, churned by cart wheels and the passage of countless feet. The buildings here were a haphazard mix: wattle-and-daub huts with sagging thatch, a few newer structures of rough-hewn timber attempting a Roman rectitude, and everywhere, the smell of damp and humanity. He passed a butcher’s stall, the air thick with the metallic scent of blood and the sweet-rot smell of offal. Flies, undeterred by the cold, clustered on a heap of discarded bones. A child, naked from the waist down, stared at him from a doorway, its eyes wide and empty.

His destination was a plot at the edge of the settlement, where the ordered grid of the Roman military encampment frayed into the chaotic British trackways. It was a strip of land he’d leased from a retired *optio* who’d married a local woman and now kept pigs. The price had been steep, but it was far from the crowded centre, far from the eyes that might, one day, turn curious and then dangerous.

There was a pile of timber under a greased hide, a mound of river stones, and the beginnings of a foundation trench, now half-full of muddy water. A simple rectangle. A workshop, with a living space at the back. Not a house. A *taberna*. He would not call it a home. Homes were for men with futures.

He dropped the sack under the hide and took up the shovel. The work was brutal, a war against the sodden earth. Each thrust of the blade was a jarring impact that travelled up his arms and into his bad shoulder. He welcomed it. The physical pain was a clean, simple thing. It drove out the other pain, the one that was a hollowed-out room in his mind where the memories lived: the cool marble of Gallus’s atrium, the sound of the fountain, the old man’s voice, dry as parchment, saying, “You must go, Lucius. Tonight. They have the list. Your name is on it.”

He had argued. A fool’s impulse. He was a freedman, yes, but Gallus’s secretary and estate manager for twenty years. His loyalty was his currency. “I stay,” he’d said.

Gallus had smiled, a thin, tired crack in his face. “And die a useless death? No. Your loyalty is noted. Now take it elsewhere and let it live. Go to Britannia. There is a man in Londinium, an old legionary. He owes me. He will get you land. Be a carpenter. Be a ghost.”

A ghost. He dug, the rhythm of it becoming a chant. *Be a ghost. Be a ghost.* The rain mingled with the sweat on his back. He worked until the light began to fail, the grey deepening to charcoal. Only then did he stop, his breath pluming in the chill air. He wiped his face with a wet forearm and looked at his work: a trench, clearer now, defining a space. A beginning. It felt less like creation and more like excavation.

From his small satchel, he took a waxed cloth bundle. Inside was a heel of hard cheese, a piece of the gritty bread, and a small, stoppered clay flask. He broke off some cheese, ate standing. Then he uncorked the flask. It held not the local swill, but a few precious mouthfuls of true *vinum*, brought from Massilia. He didn’t drink it. He poured a libation onto the muddy ground at the corner of the foundation trench. A whisper, too low for even the drizzle to hear. “*Dis Manibus.*” For the spirits of the dead. For Gallus, who was surely dead by now. For the man he had been.

A sound made him turn. A figure was approaching down the path, moving with a careful, hesitant step. A woman. British, by her dress—a long, dark gown of wool, a shawl over her head. She carried a basket. She stopped a few paces away, her eyes on the libation spot, then on his face.

“You make an offering to your gods for this place?” she asked. Her Latin was clearer than Vellocatus’s, less accented.

“I make an offering,” Lucius replied, corking the empty flask.

She nodded, as if this confirmed something. “I am Elen. My husband, Cynon, owns the land to the west.” She gestured vaguely. “He said a Roman was building here. A quiet one.”

“Lucius.”

“Just Lucius?”

“For now.”

A faint, almost imperceptible smile touched her lips. She was not young, perhaps thirty-five, with a face that was strong rather than pretty, weathered by a life outdoors. Her eyes were a peculiar shade of green-grey, like the sea under a winter sky. “It is a poor time of year to build. The ground is hungry. It swallows effort.”

“It is the time I have.”

She considered him, then her gaze swept over the trench, the piled materials, his solitary figure. “You work alone.”

“I do.”

“A man alone builds a small house. Or a large tomb.” She stepped forward, set her basket on a relatively dry stone. From it, she took a small, cloth-wrapped parcel. “Here. It is only stew. Hare and barley. It has flavour. More than you will get from your cheese.”

Lucius was taken aback. This was not Roman courtesy, the complex dance of client and patron. This was something else. Direct. Possibly dangerous. “I have nothing to trade for it.”

“I did not ask for trade,” she said. “The land is better with a neighbour who makes offerings to his dead. It shows he understands the weight of things.” She turned to go, then paused. “The nails from Vellocatus are good. But his lime is weak. He mixes it with too much sand. There is a man by the second military kiln, a Gaul. His lime is strong. It will bind.”

She walked away, back into the gloom, her figure soon swallowed by the mist and the descending night.

Lucius stood over the parcel. The cloth was still warm. He could smell it now—thyme, onion, the rich gamey scent of the hare. His mouth watered, a traitorous reaction. He had not eaten a proper meal in weeks. This was a hook, he knew. An opening. In Rome, nothing was given freely. Here, perhaps it was the same. Or perhaps it was simply stew.

He took it inside the makeshift lean-to he’d erected against a surviving wall of an older, collapsed hut. He ate it slowly, by the light of a single oil lamp. It was, as she’d said, full of flavour. It tasted of earth and herbs and smoke. It tasted like something a person would make for a family.

When he was done, he sat on his pallet, the empty bowl in his hands, listening to the rain on the hide roof. The ghost of the flavour lingered on his tongue. The hollow in his mind felt, for the first time in seventeen days, not just empty, but observed. He was not a ghost. He was a man in a damp land, eating a stranger’s stew, building a box to live in. And now, someone knew his name. Or part of it.

He blew out the lamp. In the perfect dark, the sounds of *Londinium* were distant: a dog barking, a muffled shout, the endless whisper of the river. Underneath it all, he could almost hear the echo of Rome—the rumble of carts on cobbles, the cry of the water-seller in the Subura, the laughter from Gallus’s courtyard. It wasn’t memory. It was an amputated limb, still itching.

He slept. And for the first time, he did not dream of the sea voyage, or the listing deck, or the cold fear of the unknown. He dreamed of a trench, perfectly straight, filling not with water, but with a slow, relentless tide of dark, British earth.

# Chapter 2

## **CHAPTER 2**

The Gaul’s lime was, as promised, strong. It cost him more, but when Lucius mixed it with sand and water, it produced a paste that was viciously white and clung to the trowel with a tenacity that felt like promise. He worked from first light, his breath a steady rhythm of plumes in the air. The drizzle had relented, leaving a sky the colour of a dirty fleece and a cold that bit at the tips of his ears and fingers.

He was laying the first course of river stones along the foundation trench, bedding them in the mortar, when he heard the approach of horses. Not the plodding of a single cart-horse, but the coordinated walk of several. Military. He kept his back to the path, his focus on the alignment of a flat, grey stone. The sound stopped just behind him.

“You. Builder.”

Lucius finished setting the stone, tapped it level with the handle of his trowel, and then straightened, turning slowly. There were three of them on horseback. Two were legionaries in worn but serviceable armour, their faces young, bored, pink with the cold. The man in front was an officer, a *centurion* or perhaps a *decurion* of the auxiliary cavalry. He wore a polished bronze breastplate over a scarlet tunic, a short cloak fastened at his shoulder. His face was lean, weathered, with eyes that held the flat, assessing look of a man used to processing people as either problems or irrelevances.

“Sir,” Lucius said, wiping his hands on his already-stained tunic. He kept his posture neutral, neither subservient nor challenging.

The officer’s gaze swept over the site, the materials, Lucius himself. “This is your plot?”

“It is. Leased from Marcus Vibius, retired *optio*.”

“I know Vibius. He’s fond of pigs and British beer.” The officer’s Latin was the crisp, unadorned speech of the camp. “Your name?”

“Lucius Valerius.”

“Origin?”

“Italia.” A safe, vast answer.

“Trade?”

“Carpenter. And builder.”

The officer dismounted, his boots sinking slightly into the mud. He walked past Lucius, inspecting the foundation trench. He nudged a freshly-set stone with the toe of his boot. It didn’t move. He grunted, a sound of minimal approval. “Straight lines. Roman lines. You served.”

It wasn’t a question. Lucius had long ago lost the softness of a civilian clerk. His body, though ageing, still spoke of drills, of marching, of labour. “The Twentieth Valeria, sir. A long time ago.”

“The Twentieth.” The officer turned, his interest slightly piqued. “They’re up near the wall now, freezing their balls off and pacifying Picts. What’s a veteran of the Twentieth doing down here playing with stones?”

“The legions needed younger knees, sir. I took my discharge and managed an estate. In Italia.” He layered just enough of the truth into it.

“And now you’re here.” The officer said it as a final statement, a closed loop. He was not a philosopher. He was a man who checked lists. “You have your papers? Proof of lease?”

Lucius went to his lean-to, retrieved the small, oiled leather cylinder that held his most precious possessions: his faded military diploma, granting him citizenship upon discharge, the deed of lease from Vibius, and a letter of introduction, now smudged and soft from handling, from Gallus’s man in *Londinium*. He handed them over.

The officer scanned them, his lips moving slightly as he read. He looked from the papers to Lucius and back again. The name ‘Gallus’ was on the letter of introduction. A senator’s name, even a disgraced one, carried a faint, residual power, like the smell of incense in an abandoned temple.

“Everything seems in order, Valerius.” He handed the papers back. “*Londinium* is a trading post. It grows. We need solid buildings, not wattle and straw that burns like kindling. Your work is… competent. But there are rules. The width of the street frontage must be maintained. No dumping refuse in the common drain. And the taxes. You are aware of the land tax?”

“I am.”

“See that you are. The procurator’s men are less courteous than I am.” He gave a thin smile that didn’t reach his eyes. He remounted his horse with a practised ease. “One more thing. You keep to yourself. That is wise. But this is a small place. People talk. A Roman who builds alone, who speaks to few, who pours wine on the ground… it draws questions. I have enough questions to answer from the Governor. I don’t need more from the mud of the Thames-side. Understood?”

It was a warning, neatly delivered. *We see you. Don’t make us look closer.*
“Understood, sir.”

The officer nodded, tugged his reins, and the small party moved off, hooves sucking at the mud.

Lucius watched them go until they turned a corner past a tanner’s yard. His heart, which had been a slow, cold drum in his chest, began to ease its pace. The encounter had been routine. It had gone well. And yet, the officer’s final words clung to him, colder than the damp. *People talk.*

He worked through the day with a grim focus, the mortar and stones becoming a kind of meditation. By late afternoon, the first course was complete, a pale, gritty ribbon defining the future. His back ached, his shoulder screamed, but there was a blunt satisfaction in it. He was building a fact. Something that could not be argued with, could not be exiled.

As the light began to bleed away, he saw Elen again. She was not approaching him this time, but was on the path further west, scattering grain for a small flock of scraggly chickens. He acknowledged her with the barest nod. She returned it.

Later, when he had washed his tools in a bucket of cloudy water and was considering another meal of cheese, she appeared at the edge of his plot, a wooden pail in her hand.

“You survived the centurion,” she said, setting the pail down. It was milk, still frothy from the cow.

“It seems so.”

“Decurion Metellus. He is a careful man. He likes his lists.” She said it as if reciting a known fact. “The milk is from my husband’s best cow. It is rich. You look like you could use it.”

Lucius felt a flicker of irritation. This constant, unasked-for charity. “Your husband does not mind you giving his milk to a stranger?”

“My husband is at the north pasture, seeing to a sick sheep. He will be two days. And I do not give it. I trade.”

“Ah.” Here it was. The hook. “And what do you trade for?”

“A story.”

He stared at her. “A story.”

“You are from Rome. A city of a million stories. You have served in the legions. You have managed an estate for a great man. And now you are here, in the mud, building a box. I have given you stew and now milk. I ask for a story in return. Not a secret. Just a story. One true thing about the world beyond this river and this rain.”

It was such an odd, disarming request. Not coin, not labour, not favour. A story. He looked at her face, earnest, curious, those sea-coloured eyes fixed on him. He thought of the centurion’s warning. *People talk.* But this didn’t feel like talking. It felt like something older.

He took the pail. “What kind of story?”

“Any kind. A story of a building. The greatest building you ever saw.”

He was silent for a long moment. He sat on a squared-off timber, took a sip of the milk. It was, as she said, rich and sweet. He looked past her, past the wattle fences and the smoking roofs of *Londinium*, seeing not the grey sky but a blinding blue one.

“The greatest building…” he began, his voice taking on a different texture, the Latin softening, becoming descriptive. “It wasn’t in Rome. It was in a place called Petra. A city of red rock, in the desert south of Judea. I was there with the legion, decades ago. The locals, the Nabateans, they didn’t build their greatest temple from the ground up. They found a mountain of sheer, rose-coloured stone, and they carved the temple down out of it. For generations, they carved. They started at the top and worked their way down, cutting the façade out of the cliff face. Columns, pediments, statues of gods—all from the living rock. When you approach it, you see this… this perfect, classical temple front, but it is a wound in the mountain. A beautiful wound. The portico is a hundred feet high. Inside, the chamber is cool and dark, and the only sound is the echo of your own breath. It is not a building that was made. It is a building that was revealed. They just cut away everything that wasn’t it.”

He stopped. He had not thought of the Khazneh in years. The memory was vivid: the heat shimmering off the canyon floor, the stunned silence of his fellow soldiers as they rounded the Siq and saw it glowing in the late afternoon sun.

Elen had not moved. Her expression was one of deep absorption. “They carved their god’s house from the heart of the world,” she said softly. “That is a proper way to build.”

“It is a way,” Lucius said, the present cold seeping back into his bones. “Here, we pile stone on stone.”

“It is still a revelation,” she said. “You cut away the life you had to find the one you are building now.” She stood, brushing her hands on her skirts. “The milk is yours. The story was fair trade.”

She left him then. Lucius sat in the gathering dusk, the taste of desert rock and cold British milk mingling in his mouth. She had understood the story in a way he had not intended. He had meant to describe a marvel of engineering. She had heard a parable of exile.

That night, as he lay on his pallet, he did not pour a libation. He thought of the carved temple, a thing of permanence in a sea of sand. He thought of his own little rectangle of stones, a fragile assertion of order in the sucking mud. And he thought of Elen’s green-grey eyes, watching, listening, understanding too much. The centurion saw a potential problem. She saw a story. He wasn’t sure which was more dangerous.

# Chapter 3

## **CHAPTER 3**

The dream was not of Rome, but of the ledger. He was in Gallus’s tablinum, the morning light sharp on the wax tablets. He was tallying the harvest from the Apulian estates—amphorae of oil, sacks of grain, the percentage to be paid in tax. His stylus moved, making the neat, vertical marks. But the numbers wouldn’t hold. The mark for one hundred would blur and become ten. A column of figures for wheat would dissolve into a trickle of sand. He’d look up, and Gallus would be there, but his face was featureless, smooth as an egg, and he would point a silent finger at the ledger. Lucius would look down and see the wax had become the surface of the Thames, dark and flowing, carrying away all the numbers, all the order, into the grey oblivion.

He woke with a start, the echo of the old man’s silent accusation in the room. It was just before dawn. A hard frost had silvered the world, crisping the mud, making the stones of his foundation gleam like bone. His shoulder was a knot of frozen fire.

He built a small, efficient fire with chips of wood and kindling, boiled water, and ate a porridge of the barley Elen had included with the stew. The routine was settling on him, the routine of survival. As he ate, he planned the day: the second course of stones, then the setting of the corner posts for the timber frame.

He was mixing the last of the Gaul’s lime when Cynon arrived.

Elen’s husband was a big man, taller than Lucius and broad across the chest, with a thick beard the colour of rust and watchful eyes. He wore a heavy cloak of checked wool, British style, but his tunic was Roman, and on his feet were good, hobnailed *caligae*—military boots. He stood at the edge of the plot, his hands on his hips, surveying the work.

“My wife says you are a craftsman,” Cynon said. His voice was a deep rumble, his Latin fluent but with a rhythmic cadence that was all his own.

“I am adequate,” Lucius replied, not stopping his stirring.

“She says you tell good stories.”

Lucius paused, looked up. “I told one story.”

Cynon’s expression was unreadable. He walked over, his boots crunching on the frost, and squatted by the foundation trench. He ran a thick finger along a joint, testing the mortar. He nodded, once. “Adequate is not the word. This is straight. This is true. Vibius said you paid the lease in full, in silver. No haggling.”

“The price was the price.”

Cynon stood. “A Roman who pays the price, builds straight, and does not chatter. You are either a very simple man or a very complicated one.” He fixed Lucius with his gaze. “The sheep lived. But the fence on the north pasture did not. A storm brought an old oak down. Smashed fifty feet of it. It needs repair. Proper repair, with mortised joints, not just withies and hope. I have the wood. I need the skill.”

This was it. The first real test. Not from authority, but from a neighbour. Work meant integration. It meant being seen, being relied upon, becoming part of the local economy of need and favour. It was the opposite of being a ghost.

“My own work…” Lucius began.

“Will wait. The sheep will not. I will pay you. In coin, or in meat and cheese for the winter. Your choice.”

It was a fair offer. More than fair. To refuse would be an insult, and an inexplicable one. A man building alone needed the goodwill of those around him, especially those who owned the land next door. And he needed the food.

“I will need to see the damage,” Lucius said.

“Now,” said Cynon.

The north pasture was a half-hour’s walk west, along a track that followed a sluggish, reed-choked stream. The land opened up into rolling fields, dotted with grazing sheep, their wool dirty against the frost-bleached grass. The sky was vast here, a great bowl of pressed lead. The broken fence was a sorry sight. A massive, rotten oak had given way at the roots, its trunk and crown demolishing the dry-stone wall and the wooden rail fence atop it. The sheep were contained for now by a temporary barrier of thorn bushes, but it was a feeble solution.

Lucius examined the oak, the scattered rails, the broken posts. “The stone base is sound. It’s the timber that’s gone. We can salvage some of this,” he said, kicking a split rail. “But we need new posts. You have the wood?”

Cynon pointed to a stack of freshly-cut oak trunks under a hide nearby. “I cut them last week. They are green.”

“Green is good. It’s easier to work. We’ll need to shape tenons on the posts, mortise holes in the rails. It will take two days. Maybe three.”

“You have the tools?”

“I have what I need.”

They worked in a silence that was not unfriendly, but focused. Cynon was strong and knew the land, but he was no carpenter. He followed Lucius’s instructions without complaint, hauling the heavy trunks, holding them steady as Lucius marked them with a piece of chalk. Lucius unpacked his tools—the adze, the chisels, the mallet, the bow-drill. They were good tools, Italian steel, kept sharp and oiled. They felt like extensions of his own hands, the last pieces of his old life that still had a clear, unambiguous purpose.

As Lucius began to shape the first tenon, the rhythmic *thwack* of the mallet on the chisel cutting through the quiet morning, Cynon spoke.

“You served with the Twentieth.”

“I did.”

“My father served with the Second Augusta. As an auxiliary. Fought for Claudius when he came. He got his citizenship on a field near the River Medway. He always said the Romans knew two things: how to build a straight road, and how to hold a line.”

“He was not wrong.”

“He married a woman of the Catuvellauni. My mother. I grew up with a foot in each world. It is a useful way to be, if sometimes… uncomfortable.” Cynon watched Lucius’s precise work. “My wife likes you. She says you have a weight about you. Not a bad weight. A real one.”

Lucius didn’t look up. “I am just a man building a wall.”

“Perhaps.” Cynon was silent for a while, then he said, casually, as if mentioning the weather, “Decurion Metellus came to see me yesterday. After he saw you. Asked what I knew of my new Roman neighbour.”

The mallet missed its strike, glancing off the chisel and biting a small, unnecessary gash into the oak. Lucius carefully set the tools down. “And what did you tell him?”

“I told him you leased from Vibius, you built straight, you paid in silver, and you were fixing my fence. He seemed satisfied. For now.” Cynon met his gaze. “In this place, the Romans look for trouble. The tribes look for advantage. A man who is neither one nor the other must be careful where he steps.”

“I am not looking for trouble,” Lucius said, his voice low.

“I believe you. But trouble has a way of reading lists. And following ships.” Cynon let the words hang in the cold air. He had not asked a direct question. He had offered a fact, and a warning. He had, Lucius realized, just told his own story. A man with a foot in two worlds. He understood exile.

Lucius picked up the mallet and chisel again. He repositioned them, and this time the strike was true, a clean curl of pale wood peeling away. “This tenon will hold,” he said.

They worked through the day. By late afternoon, the first three new posts were set in stone-packed holes, standing upright and true against the vast sky. The mortised rails would come tomorrow. As they packed up the tools, Cynon handed Lucius a cloth bundle. “From Elen. Bread, and some of last year’s apples. They are wrinkled, but sweet.”

Lucius took it. “Thank her for me.”

“You can thank her yourself. Come to the house for the evening meal. When the fence is done. It is not a story I offer. It is just a meal.”

It was an invitation into the hearth. A step further in. Lucius felt the old instinct to withdraw, to stay in the safe, lonely perimeter of his own making. But the man’s quiet solidarity, the unspoken understanding, demanded a different response. The ghost could not share a meal. A man could.

“I will come,” Lucius said.

The walk back to his plot felt longer, the dusk closing in faster. The frost of the morning was returning with a vengeance, a crystalline edge forming on every puddle, every blade of grass. When he reached his rectangle of stones, he saw something lying in the centre of the nascent floor.

It was a dead crow. Its black feathers were ruffled, one wing bent at an unnatural angle. It hadn’t been there when he left. It lay directly on the mortar of the first course, a small, dark blot against the white.

He approached slowly. There were no animal tracks in the frost around it. It had been placed, not dropped. He used the tip of his shovel to turn it over. No obvious wound, just the broken wing. It could have been a natural death. But the placement was too precise, too central.

He thought of the centurion’s flat eyes. *People talk.* He thought of Vellocatus the nail-maker. *Makes some wonder.* He thought of the vast, indifferent landscape, and the small, sharp eyes of those who lived in it, watching, interpreting. A dead bird on a new foundation. In Rome, it would be a dire omen, requiring purification rites. Here, what was it? A warning? A threat? A simple, morbid joke?

He scooped the crow onto the shovel and carried it to the edge of the property, flinging it into the brambles beyond the ditch. He returned to the foundation. The spot where it had lain was just a spot. The stones were cold. The mortar was set.

He built his fire, ate Elen’s bread and apples. The bread was dense and good, the apples indeed sweet. He stared into the flames, seeing not the crow, but the tenon he had carved, fitting snug into its mortise. A joint. A connection. He had accepted Cynon’s offer. He had agreed to a meal. He was allowing himself to be drawn into the fabric of this place, thread by thread.

And someone, perhaps, did not like the pattern he was making.

The complication was not the centurion’s visit, nor the physical labour, nor even the gnawing scarcity. It was this: exile was not a clean break. It was a slow seepage. The past could find you, even here, in the form of a name on a list, a question from an officer. And the present could reject you, in the form of a dead black bird laid upon your first, fragile claim to a future.

He banked the fire and went into his lean-to. The night was profoundly cold, and silent. But in the distance, from the direction of the river, he heard the clear, lonely call of a living crow, a harsh *caw* that sliced through the frozen air and then was gone, leaving only the echo of its own absence.



---

# Chapter 4

## **CHAPTER 4**

The air in the Twilight Realm did not move so much as breathe. It was a slow, tidal inhalation, heavy with the scent of damp earth and blooming fungus, of ozone and rotting leaves. In Londinium, the world was a thing of edges—stone against sky, river against bank, the hard line of a roof against the grey smear of cloud. Here, every edge was blurred. Light did not fall; it pooled, a luminescent sap leaking from fronds high above, or rose in ghostly vapours from the moss underfoot. It was a cathedral of decay and growth, immense, silent, and watchful. Alex stepped through the door from the cellar and felt the pressure change in his skull, a soft, insistent pop, as if he had submerged himself in a deep, still lake.

He stood on a path of pale, spongy wood that was not quite a bridge, arcing over a black stream where motes of light swirled like drowned stars. The trees were pillars holding up a ceiling of tangled violet canopy. No rain fell, but moisture beaded on his skin, on his tunic, on the worn leather of the satchel he carried. It was cold, but not the biting, honest cold of the frontier. This was a chill that seeped from within the wood itself, from the deep, dark heart of things. He took a step. The path gave slightly, resilient as cartilage. There was no sound of his own footfall, only the low, resonant hum of the place, a note felt in the teeth and the hollow of the chest.

He had thought, in his exhaustion and fear, that escape was a line crossed. You left one place and arrived in another. But exile, he was learning, was not a single door. It was an endless antechamber. You passed from Rome to the frontier, from the frontier to this… this between-space. Each transition thinner, more tenuous, leaving more of yourself behind in the threshold. He was a ghost in Londinium. Here, he was less than that—a smudge of breath on this heavy, living air.

He walked. Time lost its regimented pace. It might have been minutes or an hour before he noticed the vine. It hung from a branch like any other creeper, glossy and dark. But where the other luminescence was passive, this one pulsed. A slow, deliberate throb of sickly green travelled its length, from the high canopy down to where its tip brushed the moss. As Alex passed beneath, the pulse quickened. He stopped. The vine went still. He took another step. With a sound like rustling silk, it moved. Not a sway in a non-existent breeze, but a coiling, an aiming. The glowing tip lifted, orienting itself toward the warmth of him.

He backed away. The vine unlooped from its branch, lowering itself. Others nearby began to stir, their own phosphorescence brightening, a network of hungry attention awakening. He turned to run, but the path ahead was now festooned with them, a curtain of gently grasping, glowing strands. They were beautiful. They were patient. He was surrounded. The hum of the realm seemed to sharpen, to focus on this one small point of intrusion. He felt the same dull panic he’d felt facing the centurion—a calculation of futile angles, a crushing weight of alien jurisdiction.

“Don’t move.”

The voice was low, frayed at the edges, and came from his left. He froze. A figure detached itself from the deeper gloom between two vast, ribbed roots. She was tall, wrapped in layers of grey and green cloth that seemed to drink the light, and in her hands was a long staff of dark wood, tipped with a dull, greyish crystal. Her face was pale, sharp-boned, etched with a fatigue that was more than physical. Her eyes, the colour of weathered slate, were fixed not on him, but on the vines.

“They track movement. And warmth. Be a stone.”

She moved then, not toward him, but along the perimeter of the clearing. Her steps were utterly silent. She tapped her staff against the bole of the tree from which the primary vine depended. The crystal gave no flash, but a vibration went through the wood, a sub-audible *thrum*. The vine shuddered, its glow guttering. She tapped another, and another, a swift, precise circuit. With each contact, the predatory light in the vines dimmed, their seeking tips drooping, retreating. The rustling ceased. The clearing returned to its former state of watchful stillness, the vines once more inert decorations.

She leaned on her staff, regarding him. Her scrutiny was not hostile, but appraising, like a carpenter judging green timber. “You’re from the other side. The hard side.” Her accent was strange, the vowels flattened, the consonants clipped, but the Latin was intelligible.

“I… came through a door,” Alex said, his own voice too loud in the swallowing quiet.

“I know. I felt it. A tear in the weave. Small, but sharp.” She nodded back the way she had come. “This isn’t a place for standing still for long. Other things listen. Come.”

She turned and melted back into the forest. He had no choice but to follow. She moved with an ingrained knowledge of the terrain, avoiding patches of brighter moss, skirting pools of stagnant, mirrored water, her staff sometimes probing the ground ahead. He stumbled behind, feeling colossal and clumsy. The forest seemed to part for her and close behind him.

“What were they?” he asked, his breath short.

“Sap-draggers,” she said, without looking back. “They anchor to the great trees, drink the light-fluids. Your body heat is a feast. They’d have pierced your skin, siphoned the warmth out. Left you cold and empty. Not a bad way to go, as things here go. Quick. Quiet.”

Her matter-of-fact tone was more chilling than any fear in her voice could have been. It was the tone of Vellocatus discussing the price of iron, or Elen noting a crack in a pot. This was her mundane.

“My name is Alex.”

“Elara.” She offered nothing more.

They walked until the light—what passed for it—began to deepen from violet to a bruised indigo. The air grew colder. Elara stopped before what appeared to be a solid wall of interwoven thorn-branches, thick as a man’s arm. She placed her palm against it, whispered something that sounded less like words and more like the creak of wood under strain. The branches unwove themselves, twisting back with a dry, grating sigh to form an archway. Beyond was not a clearing, but a vast, hollow space within the trunk of a tree so enormous it defied comprehension. Its interior was a vertical chamber, the walls smooth and curved, glowing with a soft, amber radiance that seemed to emanate from the wood itself. A spiral ramp of living wood curled around the inner wall, leading up into darkness. A small, smokeless fire of blue-flamed fungus burned in a central hearth, and the air was dry, smelling of cedar and cured meat.

“My camp,” Elara said, as the thorn-door sealed itself behind them. “One of them.”

It was not a home. It was a refuge. A few hides lay rolled against the wall. A waterskin hung from a peg. Bundles of dried herbs and strange, withered fruits dangled from the higher ramp. There was a severe, transient order to it, the order of a soldier’s kit or a sailor’s bunk. Alex felt the lean-to on his foundation had been, by comparison, a statement of permanence.

She gestured to a spot by the fire. He sat, the warmth of the blue flames peculiar—it was light without heat, a visual comfort that did little to thaw the core-deep chill. She took a waterskin, drank, passed it to him. The water was cold and tasted of minerals and bark.

“You didn’t come here by accident,” she stated, settling opposite him, her staff across her knees. “The doors don’t work like that. Not anymore. The Veil is too weak for random passage. It takes a specific kind of… pressure. A specific kind of person.”

“The man who sent me. Cynon. He said I had a ‘knack’. For making things fit.”

Elara’s slate eyes sharpened. “Cynon. The smith. Yes. He would notice that. He works with the grain of things.” She studied Alex’s hands, calloused but unmarked by the specific scars of a tradesman. “You’re a builder. From the city-that-was.”

“I was.”

“And now you’re here. In the buffer.” She said the word with a weary finality. “This place, the Twilight Realm, it isn’t a land. It’s a seam. It lies between your world—the world of hard edges and fixed laws—and the… other. The place of raw pattern and thought. The Veil is what keeps them from bleeding into each other. It’s fraying. Wearing thin in places. Doors open where they shouldn’t. Things slip through. Both ways.”

Alex thought of the dead crow on his foundation. A small, sharp thing slipping through from one world of meaning into another. “What happens if it fails?”

“The hard and the soft would mix. Your laws of stone and mortar would dissolve. The dreams and nightmares of the other side would become solid, here, there, everywhere. It would be a chaos that would make your empire’s fall look like a change of the guard.” She poked the blue fungus with her staff. “My people—the rangers—we’ve tended the Veil for generations. We patch. We reinforce. But we are… diminished. And the tears are coming faster. We need more than maintenance now. We need a true mending.”

“The key,” Alex said, remembering Cynon’s urgent words. “He said to find the key.”

Elara nodded slowly. “A Seal-Breaker. Not a physical key. A focus. A tool of intention. It was forged long ago, when the Veil was first woven, as a failsafe. It can rebind the fundamental weave. But it was broken. Fragmented. One piece remains here, in the Twilight. The others… were lost to your side, during the great ruptures of your history. Your wars. Your migrations.” She looked at him, and for the first time, he saw something besides weariness and sharp appraisal. A faint, grim spark of necessity. “Cynon was right. To mend a thing, you must first understand how it fits together. You have the builder’s mind. You see joints. Tension. Load. The Veil is a structure. A failing one. To find the fragment here, you must learn to see its materials. You must learn to walk the currents.”

The next period of time—days, perhaps, though the unchanging amber light and the silent rhythm of Elara’s routines made measurement impossible—became a harsh apprenticeship. There was no theory, only brutal, immediate practice.

“The realm is not empty space,” Elara said, leading him out of the tree-camp at what she called ‘the still hour’. “It is full of currents. Streams of growth, streams of decay. Paths of memory, paths of forgetting. Your world has roads of stone. Ours has roads of intention. You must feel them.”

Her first lesson was perception. She had him sit, blindfolded, his hands pressed flat on the moss. “Listen with your skin. Not for sound. For pull.”

At first, he felt nothing but the cold damp and a profound foolishness. Then, gradually, a sensation emerged—not a sound, but a directionality, a subtle tugging in his blood, like iron sensing a lodestone. It was faint, insistent, pulling him toward the east.

“That,” Elara’s voice came from above him, “is the current towards the Heartwood. The oldest, strongest pulse. It is generally safe. It is also generally watched. Now. Find the one that feels like a shiver. Like nausea.”

He shifted his focus, letting the steady pull fade into the background. He sought the discord. It took longer, but he found it—a thin, vibrational ache that seemed to come from the north-west, a sensation that made his teeth feel loose and his stomach clench. A current of dissolution.

“Good,” she said, and the word was a rare coin. “That leads to a blight-pool. A place where the Veil is particularly thin. You will avoid that current unless you wish to meet things that have forgotten their own shape.”

The lessons progressed to navigation. She taught him to read the forest not by sight, but by these subliminal flows. A cluster of glowing, beautiful flowers might sit at the confluence of two benign currents, a place of rest. A seemingly clear, mossy avenue might be a channel for the nausea-current, and to walk it was to invite creeping despair and the attention of things that fed on it. She showed him how to use small, carved tokens of dead wood—inert matter—to test a path, tossing them ahead to see if they were tugged, repelled, or began to softly decay.

He was clumsy. He misread the flows, once stepping into a patch of calm-looking ground that was a sinkhole of forgetting. For a few terrifying minutes, he could not recall his own name, or why he was cold, staring at his hands as if they belonged to a stranger. Elara dragged him back by his belt, her grip iron, and made him chew a bitter root that brought the memories crashing back, sharp and painful.

“The realm doesn’t care if you are lost,” she said, her voice merciless. “It only *is*. Your sentiment, your fear, your hope—these are just flavours to it. You must be neutral as stone. Precise as a join.”

He began to understand. This was not magic as the priests of Rome spoke of it—divine favour, grand miracles. This was a craft. A brutal, demanding craft of perception and alignment. It was joinery on a cosmic scale. The tenon and mortise were currents of influence; the mallet was will; the glue was attention. His builder’s mind, trained to see stress and fit, began to adapt. He could now look at a grove and see not trees, but a knot of conflicting pulls, a junction to be navigated with the same caution he’d use crossing a swollen river on slippery stones.

At her tree-camp, she taught him the rudiments of the ranger’s craft. How to prepare the smokeless fungus-fire. How to draw the moisture from food using specific currents of dry air. How to weave a temporary shelter from thorn-fronds by persuading them, through a low hum, that they wished to grow in a particular shape. It was all manipulation. A dialogue with a world that was only half-material.

One evening, as they shared a meal of tough, salty dried meat and a paste of pulped nuts, Alex asked the question that had been gnawing at him. “Your people. The rangers. Where are they?”

Elara was silent for a long time, staring into the cold blue fire. “Gone. Scattered. Or lost to the currents. We were a diaspora before your people had the word for it. Tending a border no one else sees, for a people who no longer exist. Some grew weary. Some followed a current of longing so deep it took them beyond the Veil, into the other side, and they did not return. Some were taken by the things that slip through the tears.” She looked at him. “I am the last in this sector. Perhaps the last anywhere. The work continues until it cannot.”

He heard in her words the echo of his own exile. Not a clean break, but a slow seepage. A duty to a failing edifice, performed alone. He was a Roman without Rome, building a wall in the mud. She was a ranger without a people, patching a dream in the dark. They were both joiners in a world coming apart at the seams.

“The fragment of the Seal-Breaker,” he said. “Where is it?”

“It is held in a place called the Echoing Cavern,” she said. “A place where the Veil is so thin it acts as a mirror, not a wall. It reflects what is strongest in you. Your memories. Your fears. Your obsessions. To reach the fragment, you must walk through your own reflection without being consumed by it. The cavern is fed by a current of pure remembrance. It is the most dangerous path of all.”

She saw the understanding dawn on his face, and the dread that followed it. To walk through his own past. The centurion’s list. The cold shore of exile. The dead crow on the stones. The lonely, echoing *caw* in the frozen dark.

“That is the next step,” Elara said, her voice not unkind, but inexorable. “You have learned to feel the currents. Tomorrow, you will learn to walk the strongest one of all. The one that leads back into yourself.”

That night, in the amber gloom of the tree-camp, Alex lay on a stiff hide and could not sleep. The blue fire cast no comforting shadows. He thought of the foundation he had left behind, the mortar setting without him. He thought of Cynon and Elen, faces already taking on the quality of portraits, small and distant. He had fled one form of capture only to be enlisted into another, deeper service. He was being woven into a different fabric, the pattern of which was salvation or doom on a scale he could barely grasp.

And he understood now the true nature of the complication. It was not that the past could find you. It was that you carried the past within you, a tangled, living root-system. To do what Elara asked, to mend the Veil, he would have to descend into that thicket. He would have to face not just the memory of exile, but the precise grain of its loneliness, the exact weight of the ash-grey rain, the specific scent of the river mud on the day he knew he would never go home.

The realm outside was silent in its endless, breathing night. But within him, the current began to pull, a slow, sure, terrifying tide, drawing him not east or west, but inward, toward the cavern where everything he had fled was waiting, reflected and amplified, holding the one fragment that might save two dying worlds.



---

# Chapter 5

## **CHAPTER 5**

The Heartwood did not begin with a border. It began with a silence. The constant, susurrating chorus of the sentinel trees—the clicks, the sighs, the low hum of sap through petrified veins—it all fell away over the span of twenty paces, as if a door had been shut on a crowded room. The air lost its taste of ozone and damp moss. It became still, and thin, and carried a new scent: the dry, clean aroma of sun on stone, of a place that had forgotten the concept of rain.

Alex stopped, his boots sinking into a carpet of pine needles that were not black, but a deep, rusted gold. Elara stood beside him, her usual fluid stillness replaced by a taut, listening rigidity. They had walked for a day since the conversation by the blue fire, a day of silent preparation through increasingly watchful woods. Now, the forest ahead was different. The trees were older, their trunks wider than the roundhouses of his village, their bark the texture of cracked granite. Light did not filter down here; it was generated, a soft, sourceless amber glow that pooled in the clearings and clung to the boughs like gossamer. It was the light of late afternoon, perpetually held at the moment before dusk.

“We have crossed the verge,” Elara said, her voice low, respecting the quiet. “The rules are different here. The Heartwood remembers everything that has ever fallen here—a leaf, a drop of rain, a life. It does not forget. And it… reflects.”

“The current of remembrance,” Alex said. The words felt too loud.

“The same river, but here it runs above ground. Do not touch the water we must cross. Do not even look too long. Your memories are your own. In there,” she gestured ahead, to where the golden light thickened between the endless, columnar trees, “they can become everyone’s.”

He wanted to ask what she saw when she looked into that water. What memories a woman who was centuries old, guardian of a dying world, would need to resist. But her profile was closed, a monument facing a familiar storm. He saw, for the first time, not a guide, but a fellow exile. Her diaspora was not of place, but of time. She was the last of her kind, living amidst the echoes of what was lost. The understanding was a cold stone in his gut. This was the fellowship of the displaced: a shared language of silence, of carrying a lost world in your posture, in the careful way you held a tool no one else remembered how to use.

“The Spire of Lyrion,” he said, forcing them forward. “That is where the shard is?”

“It is where the shard *fell*,” she corrected. “A needle of pure crystal, driven into the Heartwood’s core in an age before the Veil. It is a… lodestone. For power. For attention. The fragment we seek was drawn to it. It is also the most dangerous place in this realm. The currents converge there. It is a place of pure seeing.”

“And to get there, we walk through our own pasts.”

“We walk *past* them,” she said, turning her grey eyes on him. “There is a difference. You are a builder. You understand foundation. What is below must be acknowledged, but it must not undermine the structure you are trying to raise. That is the task. Acknowledge. Do not be undermined.”

It was the closest she had come to a profession of faith in him. He nodded, a centurion accepting orders before a siege. The dread was still there, the cold tide pulling inward, but he had a tool now, a purpose: build a path through it. Mend the break. He formally accepted the quest not with words, but by adjusting the pack on his shoulders, by taking the first firm step into the amber glow of the Heartwood. The silence swallowed the sound of his footsteps whole.

***

They followed a path that was not a path, a gentle declivity between the monumental trees. The golden light seemed to pulse with a slow, sleeping rhythm. After an hour, Alex heard it: the sound of water. Not a rush or a babble, but a low, resonant *thrum*, a cello note played endlessly beneath the earth. The air grew damp again, but the scent was wrong—it was the smell of wet parchment, of old ink, of a room where letters from the dead are stored.

The trees parted, revealing the river.

It was not wide, perhaps ten paces across, but it flowed with a viscous, deliberate slowness, its surface a perfect, mirror-bright silver. It did not reflect the amber trees or the soft glow above. It reflected nothing of the world around it. Instead, as Alex approached, careful to stay on the bank of smooth, white stone, images bloomed just beneath its metallic skin.

He saw the grey smear of Londinium’s rain on the sleeve of his cloak. Not a memory, but the thing itself, every droplet precise. He saw the chandler’s sign creaking in the wind, the grain of the water-warped wood. He heard the curse of a cart driver as his wheel sank into the mud. The sensations were not recalled; they were *reissued*, fresh and immediate. The cold seeped into his bones anew. The loneliness was a fist around his throat.

He tore his gaze away, breathing hard. “It pulls.”

“It does not pull. It offers,” Elara said. She was staring resolutely at a fixed point on the opposite bank. “It offers the comfort of the lived-in past. The known quantity. For an exile, it is the most potent temptation. To dwell in the memory of home is to be home, for a moment. But the moment stretches. And you become a reflection yourself, trapped in a single scene, while the river flows on without you.”

Ahead, a series of broad, flat stones formed a natural bridge across the silver current. As Alex watched, the surface of the river directly below the first stone shimmered. The image of a dead crow materialized, its feathers glossy and wet, one milky eye staring up. The *caw* echoed, not from the trees, but from the water itself, a hollow, freezing sound that rooted him to the spot. It was the precise grain of his exile’s dread, given form.

“Do not look down,” Elara commanded, her voice a whip-crack in the heavy air. “Your eyes are on the far bank. Your mind is on the Spire. Your feet are stone. They are only memories. They have no weight unless you give it to them.”

She stepped onto the first stone. The water beneath her feet remained blank, silver, inert. She had mastered her currents, or had none so bitter to offer.

Alex forced a foot forward. The stone was solid. He took another step. The crow’s corpse swirled away, replaced by another scene: Cynon, by the hearth in the roundhouse, his face etched with a disappointment so profound it was worse than anger. The old man’s lips moved, soundless, but Alex knew the words. *You were meant to build here. You were meant to stay.* The guilt was a physical heat on his face.

He froze, halfway across. The silver water lapped at the edges of the stone, though there was no wind. It wanted to touch him. It wanted to pull him into the gallery of his own regrets.

“Alex.” Elara’s voice came from ahead. She had reached the other side. “The foundation. You are building it now. The next stone. Only the next stone.”

He was a builder. He built things. He did not dwell in things that were broken. He fixed them. This was a broken path. He had to fix it, stone by stone. He wrenched his gaze from Cynon’s fading face and fixed it on the next pale slab. He stepped. The image beneath shattered into meaningless silver ripples.

The next stone showed Elen, turning away from him in the village square. The next, the empty chair where his father had once sat. The next, the cold, featureless wall of the barracks in Rome, the smell of male sweat and ambition. He walked through a museum of his leaving, each exhibit meticulously curated to wound. But he kept his feet moving, his breath a ragged chant in his ears. *Acknowledge. Do not be undermined.*

The final stone was the largest. The water below it was dark, a pool of ink. As his foot touched it, the image did not bloom—it erupted.

It was the shore of his exile. Not a memory, but the totality of it. The crunch of shingle under his boots, the salt and sewage stink of the Tiber estuary, the receding trireme that had dumped him here, the vast, flat, grey expanse of sky that promised nothing. The utter and absolute *end* of his story. The loneliness was not a fist now; it was the air itself, thick and unbreathable. He could feel the grit of the ash-grey rain on his skin, the damp wool of his cloak, the hollow, starving ache of a future erased.

He gasped, doubling over on the stone. The compulsion to look closer, to step into that scene, to own it fully because it was the last true thing he had possessed, was nearly overwhelming. It was his. It had forged him. Why not embrace it? Why not live forever in the moment of his making?

“Because it is a tomb.”

Elara’s voice was not beside him. It was inside him, clear and cold as a spring. “A tomb you have been carrying. Put it down. The man who steps off that stone is not the man who stepped onto it. Or the journey is for nothing.”

With a sob that was part rage, part agony, he pushed away from the water, from the shore, from the ghost of the man standing on it. He took the last, stumbling step onto the far bank and collapsed onto the rust-gold needles, his back to the terrible, silver river.

He had not been consumed. But he felt flayed. Hollowed out. The memories had weight, and he had carried every ounce across.

Elara did not offer a hand. She let him breathe, let the solid, unchanging earth of the Heartwood press against him. When his shuddering subsided, she said, “You see now. The past is not behind us. It is a country we inhabit. We are all exiles from the present, to some degree. The skill is in governing that country, not being governed by it.”

He pushed himself up. The amber light seemed harsher. The silence more profound. He felt, irrevocably, that he had passed a boundary within himself. The tangled root-system was still there, but he had seen its shape. He had walked through its darkest thicket and emerged, scarred, but whole.

“Good,” Elara said, reading his face. “Now we move. The river’s call will have been a signal. It draws us. It draws others.”

***

The landscape beyond the river did not change so much as *intensify*. The colossal trees grew closer together, their roots forming great, arched buttresses that created tunnels and caverns of smooth, living wood. The amber light was stronger, emanating from the bark itself, casting long, dramatic shadows that seemed to move just at the edge of vision. The air thrummed with a low, sub-auditory power, a heartbeat that was not his own. This was no longer simply a forest. It was a cathedral, ancient and aware, built to principles of geometry and power he could not comprehend.

The rules were older here. Time felt granular, pooling in some places, rushing in others. He saw a cluster of luminous fungi pulse through a full day’s cycle of growth, decay, and rebirth in the span of a dozen heartbeats. A stream of clear water trickled over a rock, and as he watched, the water flowed *upwards*, defying the stone, before resuming its normal course. The very ground seemed to breathe, a gentle, slow expansion and contraction beneath the needle floor.

Elara moved with heightened caution, her eyes scanning not just the path, but the quality of the shadows, the pitch of the silence. She had drawn her long, pale knife. Alex hefted his walking staff, feeling its mundane oak inadequacy.

“The Heartwood’s memory is perfect,” she whispered, her voice barely disturbing the air. “It remembers every creature that has ever walked here. Sometimes, it… replays them. Do not trust every shape you see. Trust nothing that does not cast a shadow in this light.”

They passed a glade where a spectral herd of great, elk-like creatures with antlers of woven light grazed on glowing moss. The beasts flickered, insubstantial as mist. A memory, playing itself out on the stage of the wood. Further on, they saw the figure of a man in robes of a forgotten style, kneeling before a tree, his form repeating the same series of motions like a clockwork toy. Alex felt a profound, aching sadness looking at him—another exile, trapped in the echo of a single, significant moment.

He was so focused on these ghostly tableaux that he almost missed the real threat.

It was the absence of sound that warned him. A patch of the forest floor, just off their intended route, where the gentle breathing of the earth had stopped. Where the amber light seemed to shy away, creating a pool of deeper gloom.

He grabbed Elara’s arm, stopping her. He pointed, not with his hand, but with a tilt of his chin, the way soldiers did on patrol. She followed his gaze, and her body went wire-tight.

From the gloom, the shadows detached themselves.

They were not memories. They cast no shadows because they were shadows themselves, forms woven from concentrated darkness and a palpable, hungry malice. They were humanoid in shape, but elongated, their movements a liquid stutter, as if they crossed the space between heartbeats. There were four of them. They carried no weapons; their hands ended in long, sharp shards of darkness that drank the light. Their faces were smooth, blank ovals, featureless save for a faint, ripple-like pattern that suggested a surface under pressure.

“Reavers,” Elara breathed, the word a curse. “They have found the scent.”

“What are they?”

“Scavengers of the rift. They believe the Veil’s thinning is a liberation. They seek to tear it down completely, to let the realms bleed into one another in chaos. They feed on the discord. They want the fragment—not to mend, but to use as a wedge.”

The nearest Reaver turned its blank face toward them. A sound emanated from it, not a voice, but the psychic impression of one: the screech of tearing metal, the groan of a structure failing under immense strain. It was the sound of a world coming apart.

Then they moved.

There was no warning rush. One moment they were twenty paces away, pooling in the gloom; the next, they were *between* the spaces, flowing around the great tree trunks like black oil. The air turned ice-cold in their wake.

“Run!” Elara shouted, shoving him sideways, off the faint path.

They ran, not with the hope of outpacing them, but of outmaneuvering. The Heartwood’s impossible geometry became their only ally. Alex followed Elara as she darted through a narrow crevice between two fused roots, scrambled up a slope of soft, phosphorescent lichen, and dropped down into a hollow where the air was thick and sweet with the smell of decaying blossoms.

The Reavers did not follow so much as *reappear*, cutting off angles, herding them. They were silent hunters, their only sound that terrible, structural scream they projected into the mind. Alex felt it in his teeth, in the marrow of his bones. It was the antithesis of building. It was the essence of collapse.

One materialized directly in front of them, its blank face rippling. Elara didn’t break stride. She lunged, her pale knife flashing. It passed through the creature’s torso as if through smoke, but where the blade touched, a crackle of white light spiderwebbed through the darkness. The Reaver recoiled, its form shuddering, emitting a burst of that mental screech that made Alex cry out in pain. It was vulnerable to something—to her, to the knife, to the power it carried.

“They are creatures of unmade things!” Elara yelled, spinning to face another that oozed from the trunk of a tree. “They have no solid past! Your memories—your *solid* past—it disrupts them!”

It made a desperate, insane kind of sense. They were the opposite of the river. They were the void where memory should be. He had just walked through a gallery of his most solid, painful truths.

Another Reaver flowed toward him, its clawed hand slicing at his face. He ducked, swinging his oak staff. It passed through the shadowy limb with no resistance, leaving him off-balance. The creature’s other hand raked toward his chest. On instinct, he didn’t try to block. He focused.

He focused on the weight of the ash-grey rain on his cloak in Londinium. The specific, gritty feel of it. The smell of the river mud, thick and primeval. The exact curve of the dead crow’s claw against the wet stone.

He held the memory, not as a nostalgia, but as a fact. As a foundation stone.

The shadow-hand passed into the space over his heart—and met resistance. A faint, silver shimmer, like the ghost of the river, flickered over his tunic. The Reaver’s hand stopped, as if plunged into setting mortar. It writhed, trying to pull free, the ripples on its face becoming frantic.

Elara was at his side in an instant. Her knife plunged into the creature’s core, and this time, the white light did not spiderweb—it *erupted*. The Reaver came apart in a soundless burst of dissipating smoke and a final, fading shriek of tearing metal.

“Yes!” she said, her eyes alight with fierce triumph. “Use it! Your exile is your armor here! It is a truth they cannot digest!”

The remaining two Reavers hesitated, their fluid forms coiling and uncoiling. The rules had shifted. The prey had become poisonous.

Alex stepped forward, not away. He reached into the thicket, not to flee it, but to wield it. He thought of the cold shore, the receding ship. He thought of the list with his name on it, the scrape of the stylus. He thought of Cynon’s disappointment, and Elen’s turned back. He gathered the sharp, hard stones of his diaspora and held them in his mind, polished and undeniable.

He looked at the nearest Reaver and *remembered*, forcefully, deliberately.

The creature flinched, a shudder passing through its darkness. It began to lose cohesion, its edges blurring like ink in water.

A sharp whistle cut the air. Not from Elara. From deeper in the wood. The two remaining Reavers instantly dissolved into pools of shadow that seeped into the forest floor and vanished. The crushing, silent pressure lifted. The amber light returned to its full strength. The heartbeat of the earth resumed its slow thrum.

Elara stood panting, her knife held low. “They are recalled. Their master is near.”

“Master?”

Before she could answer, a new figure emerged from between the trees. He was tall, clad in robes of deep forest green that seemed woven from living moss and shadow. His face was sharp, ageless, his eyes the colour of the silver river, reflective and depthless. He carried no weapon. He simply observed them, his expression one of cold, academic interest.

“Elara of the Last Vigil,” the man said, his voice smooth as worn stone. “You are far from your dying tree. And you bring a curiosity.” His silver eyes settled on Alex. “A mortal. Stuffed with so many little sorrows. You used them quite effectively. Like throwing sand in a mechanism.”

“Lyrion,” Elara said, and the name was a shield and a challenge in one.

“A namesake only. A disciple.” The man—Lyrion—smiled, a thin curve of the lips. “The Spire remembers its maker, and we who serve it take his name as our purpose. You seek the fragment.”

“To mend what your kind seeks to break.”

“To mend?” Lyrion laughed, a dry, rustling sound. “You preserve a corpse. The Veil is a scar. My master understood that true power lies not in separation, but in synthesis. In the glorious, chaotic confluence of all things. The fragment you seek is a key. You would use it to lock the door. We would use it to break the frame.”

He took a step closer. Alex felt a pressure on his mind, not the screaming collapse of the Reavers, but a probing, insistent force, seeking cracks. He clenched his memories around him like a cloak of chainmail.

“He is stronger than he looks,” Lyrion mused. “A foundation of regrets. How… Roman of him. But everything erodes, Sentinel. Even memory. Even resolve.”

He raised a hand. The amber light around them dimmed, drawn toward him. The very memory of the light seemed to falter. Alex felt a terrifying slippage in his own mind, a softening of the hard edges of his past.

“Run,” Elara whispered, but this time it was a plea, not a command. “To the Spire. It is close. Its light will disrupt his working. Go!”

She lunged at Lyrion, her knife blazing with white fire. The disciple flicked his wrist, and a wall of twisted, thorned shadows erupted from the ground to meet her.

Alex ran.

He ran not from fear, but toward the only objective left. He crashed through the glowing undergrowth, following a sudden, magnetic pull in his blood, a resonance with the fragment he had carried. The amber world blurred around him. He heard the clash of light and shadow behind him, the sizzle of Elara’s power against the corrosive dark.

The trees opened up.

He stumbled into a clearing so vast it could have held his entire village. And at its centre, rising from a mound of pure, crystalline rock, was the Spire of Lyrion.

It was not a tower of stone, but a single, impossible shard of clear crystal, taller than the tallest tree, thrust into the heart of the mound like a blade. It caught the sourceless light and fractured it, throwing rainbows and spears of pure white illumination in a slow, rotating dance across the clearing. The air hummed with a high, clean note that vibrated in his teeth. This was the lodestone. The place of pure seeing.

And there, halfway up the crystalline mound, embedded in the rock and pulsing with a soft, familiar blue light, was the fragment. His fragment. The other half of the key.

He took a step toward it. Then another.

A figure stepped from behind the Spire’s base. It was another of the green-robed disciples, a woman with silver hair and those same reflective eyes. She held a curved blade of dark metal.

“The Sentinel delays our master,” she said, her voice echoing the high hum of the Spire. “But the mechanism has many parts. You will not reach the shard, little mortal. Your memories will be unspooled, and you will be left empty, a vessel for the new truth.”

Alex stood at the edge of the clearing, the immense Spire before him, the fragment calling, an enemy in his path, and the sounds of Elara’s battle fading behind. The current that had pulled him inward had brought him here, to the fulcrum. He was an exile from two worlds now, stranded in the space between. He had no army, no legion. He had only the weight of the ash-grey rain, the cold of the shore, the taste of river mud, and the stubborn, building hands of a man who refused to let things fall apart.

He hefted his simple oak staff. He fixed the solid, unassailable memory of his father’s hands showing him how to set a true cornerstone in his mind.

“Try,” he said.

And he began to walk across the radiant clearing, toward the Spire, toward the fragment, toward the end of his exile or its final, perfect shape.



---

# Chapter 6

## **CHAPTER 6**

The refuge was not a place, but a silence. After the high, clean hum of the Spire and the metallic shriek of Elara’s battle, the world here was muffled, packed in wool. They had fled not into the forest, but through it, following a path that was less a trail than a suggestion of less resistance between the ancient, moss-backed stones. The rain here was the London kind, not the Roman; a fine, persistent ash that settled into the fibres of their cloaks and the furrows of the bark, a grey exhalation from a sodden sky. It seeped, it didn’t fall. Alex felt it in his joints, a cold reminder of the river mud and the weight of exile.

Elara moved ahead of him, a shadow among shadows, her breathing a controlled rhythm that betrayed nothing but the occasional catch of a bruised rib. The Sentinel’s blade was dark with a sap-like ichor. She had found him just as he’d reached the base of the crystalline mound, the silver-haired disciple disarmed and unconscious at his feet, his own staff split from parrying her curved blade. There had been no time to claim the fragment. A tremor had gone through the Spire, the hum spiking into a painful whine, and more of the green robes had emerged from the trees, their reflective eyes blank as polished slate.

“Run,” Elara had said, and it was not a suggestion.

They ran. For hours, it felt like, until the light lost all quality and became a mere thinning of the gloom. The forest gave way to a sunken valley, a bowl of land hidden from the winds where the trees grew twisted and vast, their canopies knitting a roof against the ash-rain. And there, nestled among the great roots as if the earth had coughed them up, were the dwellings.

They were not huts. They were built of the same grey, lichen-crusted stone as the forest floor, low and rounded, their roofs thick with living sod. No smoke rose from them. No paths connected them. They seemed less a community than a collection of afterthoughts, a diaspora of structures. The only sign of habitation was the light—a cool, blue-white glow that emanated from within, casting no flickering shadows, but a steady, moon-like radiance from behind thick panes of what looked like oiled parchment.

Elara stopped at the edge of the clearing. “The Quiet House,” she said, her voice low. “Scholars of the Veil. They see much. They say little.”

“Are they friendly?” Alex asked, his own voice rough. The memory of the Spire’s hum still vibrated in his back teeth.

“They are… apart. Exiles by choice. They care only for the texture of reality, not the wars fought upon it. But they owe the Sentinels a debt. It will grant us a night. Perhaps some answers.”

They were met at the door of the central dwelling not by a person, but by a presence. The door, a slab of age-blackened wood, swung inward without a hand touching it. The air that escaped was dry and carried a scent Alex had not encountered since his first life: the smell of vellum, of crushed lavender, of slow, undisturbed dust. It was the smell of a library in a forgotten wing of a palace. It was the antithesis of wet dog and river mud.

Inside, the light came from glass orbs set in niches, filled with a liquid that glowed with that same steady, cold fire. The room was a labyrinth of shelves, but these shelves held no books. They held fragments. A shard of pottery with a glimmer of gilt still clinging to it. A twist of rusted iron that might have been a key. A piece of birch bark inscribed with symbols that hurt the eyes to follow. A single, perfect feather that seemed to drink the light rather than reflect it. Between the shelves, figures moved in robes the colour of the stone outside. They did not look up. Their footsteps made no sound.

An old man emerged from between two towering cases. His head was shaved, his face a map of fine lines. His eyes were the colour of a winter sky, clear and distant. He did not look at Alex, but at the space just beside him, as if observing a ripple in the air.

“Elara of the Stone Shore,” the old man said. His voice was dry parchment rubbing together. “The debt is acknowledged. One night. The silence is yours to share, but not to break. You may look. Do not touch.”

“We need to understand the Spire of Lyrion,” Elara said, dispensing with greeting. “Its guardians. Its mechanisms.”

The old man—Alex thought of him as the Librarian—turned those pale eyes on her. “The Spire is not a thing to be understood. It is a condition. A point of clarity. The guardians are not its defenders. They are its… calibrations. Removing impurities from the lens.” He shifted his gaze, finally, to Alex. It was like being scanned by a beam of cold light. “You are an impurity. You carry a fragment of elsewhere. The Spire will seek to dissolve you, to unspool the knot of your contradictory memory. That is its function.”

Alex felt a chill that had nothing to do with his damp clothes. “The woman with the blade spoke of unspooling memories.”

“A crude metaphor for a precise process,” the Librarian said, turning and gliding back between the shelves. He led them to a nook where a large, flat stone served as a table. Upon it was a mosaic, made not of tile but of countless chips of coloured glass and polished stone. It depicted the Spire, but around it swam nebulous, abstract shapes. “The Spire perceives all possible pasts, all branching memories of a thing. It resolves them into one, true history. The ‘new truth’ your enemy seeks. The disciples are its agents, aligning reality to that singular vision. Your memory, exile, is a particularly stubborn knot. A past that refuses to be smoothed away.”

He pointed a bony finger at one of the swirling shapes near the mosaic’s Spire. It was a knot of dark and light chips, tangled. “The guardians you faced are the outer ward. Simple filters. To reach your fragment, you must pass the Interior Ward. The Sentinel delays the master of the disciples, but the Interior Ward is autonomous. A mechanism of the Spire itself.”

“What is it?” Alex asked.

The Librarian’s lips thinned. “It is the Maze of Lamentation. It does not test your strength. It feeds on the coherence of your self. On your fears, your regrets, the memories you have failed to reconcile. It will show you the other paths, the lives you might have lived, the people you might have been. It will offer them to you, whole and perfect. To choose one is to be dissolved into it. To become a ghost in a might-have-been. The only way through is to hold your exile in both hands. To look upon the life you did not live, the man you are not, and say, ‘That is not my stone. This is.’”

Alex stared at the tangled knot in the mosaic. He thought of his father’s hands, sure on the trowel. He thought of the grey rain on the Thames. Two truths, equally solid. Both his. The ash-settled cold of London was as real as the sun on the Forum. To deny one was to become a ghost. He understood now. The Maze wouldn’t attack him. It would seduce him.

“How do we pass it?” Elara’s voice was hard, practical.

“You do not,” the Librarian said simply. “Your self is singular, Sentinel. Your path was chosen at your forging. Your regrets are for duty, not for identity. The Maze would have little purchase on you. You would wander until you starved, a rock in a stream of phantoms.” He looked back at Alex. “He must lead. And he cannot do it alone. He requires a guide who knows the texture of unreconciled memory. A fellow exile of the diaspora.”

He raised a hand, and a figure detached itself from the shadows of a far shelf.

He was younger than the Librarian, perhaps of an age with Alex. His hair was a tangled brown, his face sharp and intelligent, but set in a permanent cast of sceptical weariness. He wore the same stone-coloured robe, but his was tied carelessly, stained with what looked like ink and tree resin. In his hands he held a fragment of a clay tablet, tracing the cuneiform with a fingertip. His eyes, when he looked up, were a startling, vivid green, and they held none of the distant chill of the others. They were sharply, irritably present.

“This is Kael,” the Librarian said. “He is our best and most troublesome student. He understands the architecture of traps, the grammar of dead languages, and the precise flavour of regret. He is also exiled from this place, as of now. His curiosity has become a liability. He will go with you.”

Kael’s mouth quirked. “Thrown out for asking the wrong questions. Again.” His voice was a dry rasp, edged with a cynical humour. He tossed the tablet fragment onto a shelf. It clattered, a shockingly loud sound in the silence, making several other scholars flinch. “They don’t like it when you ask why a mechanism of pure seeing needs to be protected by a maze that deals in lies. It implies a flaw in the lens.”

Elara assessed him, her gaze flat. “Can you navigate the Maze?”

“I can read its instructions,” Kael said, shrugging. “The Maze is a text. A painful, personal, screaming text, but a text. Its walls are built from memory-shards. Its traps are syllogisms of fear. I can’t walk your path for you,” he said to Alex. “But I can point out the punctuation. Maybe keep you from walking headlong into a particularly nasty paragraph about your father.”

Alex felt a jolt. “What do you know of my father?”

“Nothing,” Kael said, his green eyes glinting. “But everyone has a paragraph about their father. It’s the first chapter in the book of regrets.” He looked between them, his cynicism giving way to a spark of raw, intellectual hunger. “The Spire of Lyrion. I’ve only ever studied the fragments of fragments about it. You’re going to the source. That’s the only question worth asking. I’m in.”

***

The ash-rain had not stopped. It coated the world in a damp, grey film as they moved out from the sunken valley at first light. The Quiet House had given them supplies: hard, tasteless cakes that seemed to dissolve on the tongue, and water drawn from a deep, still well that tasted of stone. No farewells were offered. The door simply closed behind them, sealing the silence back inside.
