He looked at his cup. The wine was still. In its dark red surface, he saw a reflection. It was his face, but younger, unlined. Yet the eyes… the eyes held a shadow. A fleck of something that did not belong in this perfect light. A memory of a different weight.

A grey smear settling on wool. The grit of bad bread between his teeth. The sound of Kaela’s voice, saying his name not as a blessing, but as a warning.

*Grit*.

The word was a pebble in the still pond of the paradise. It caused a ripple. The image in the wine shimmered. For a second, he saw not his youthful face, but his own face—weathered, tired, marked by a life of choices. The face of a man who had carried his exile like a weapon and a wound.

The table under the olive tree flickered. For a nanosecond, he saw the spherical room, the glowing crystal, his own body suspended in the air, hand outstretched. Kaela was a blur of motion on the edge of the field.

The song sensed the discord. The father’s hand on his shoulder tightened, not in comfort, but in containment. The warmth became a pressure.

“Do not grasp at the fragments,” the father said, his voice still kind, but now with an undertone of vast, impersonal urgency. “They are phantoms. Pain is a phantom. Let it go. Be whole.”

The piazza intensified. The colours became more vivid, the smells more intoxicating, the sun’s warmth more enveloping. It was love as an anesthetic. It was home as a consummation.

Leo looked away from the wine cup. He looked at his own hand, resting on the sun-warmed stone of the table. It was smooth. It had never held a weapon. It had never gripped a console as a ship tore itself apart. It had never touched Kaela’s face in a moment of shared, desperate hope.

It was a lie.

A perfect, beautiful, seamless lie.

And he was a man made of seams. Of scars. Of mismatched memories. He was a patchwork of a dead Rome and a dying ship and a grey city under a dome. He was grit.

To accept the perfection was to erase himself. Not to die, but to be *unmade*. His exile would end because the man who had been exiled would cease to be. The diaspora would be resolved by annihilating every unique, aching, stubborn point of light that had been scattered.

He closed his eyes in the sun-drenched piazza. He did not think of home. He thought of the threshold. He thought of the control room, the frozen Steward, the last hook of his own name. He thought of Kaela, her face smudged with soot, alive with conflict. *Alive*.

He summoned the grit.

He remembered the taste of the bad bread. He leaned into the memory, focused on the crunch between his molars, the annoyance of it. He remembered the damp that seeped into his cloak in Londinium, the way it chilled the bones. He remembered the hollow, metallic fear in his gut the first time he heard the song, not as a lullaby, but as a threat. He remembered Kaela’s first words to him, all suspicion and survival: “You’re not from here. Your pain is too old.”

The memories were jagged. They were dissonant. They were *his*.

The piazza flickered again, like a faulty projection. The father’s hand was now a vise.

“You cling to sickness,” the voice said, and it was no longer just his father’s. It was a chorus, the whisper of a million gathered souls, gentle and implacable. “It defines you. Let it go. Be free.”

“No,” Leo said. The word was a crack in the perfect air. It was the first true sound he had made here. It was ugly. “This is not freedom. This is quiet.”

He opened his eyes and pushed back from the table. The motion was slow, difficult, as if moving through syrup. The paradise resisted him. It loved him too much to let him go.

He looked his father in the eye. “You are not him. He is dead. I mourned him. The mourning is mine.”

He turned his back on the table, on the olive tree, on the smiling, empty faces. He began to walk, not toward the colonnade, but toward the edge of the piazza, where the perfect stone met a blur of soft, golden light. The song’s edge.

The chorus rose in a wave of soothing concern. It wrapped around him, a blanket of lullabies. It showed him other scenes. A quiet room with a view of stars. A lover’s embrace that asked for nothing. A child’s hand in his. Each one tailored, perfect, a cure for a specific loneliness.

He walked through them. He held onto the grit. The grit was his anchor. The grit was his self.

The golden blur at the edge of the piazza grew closer. The father’s voice was behind him, pleading now. “Lucius. Do not choose the void. Choose the song. Choose us.”

Leo did not answer. He fixed his mind on a single, gritty sensation: the feel of plastique in his hands, its oily, inert density. The weight of the charges in Kaela’s pack. The plan. The ugly, violent, necessary plan.

He stepped into the golden blur.

***

The spherical room snapped back into existence. The silence was a physical slap. He was still suspended in the gravity field, his hand pressed flat against the crystal.

But the crystal was no longer just glowing. It was *reacting*.

Where his palm met the surface, a darkness spread. Not a crack, but a stain. A bloom of dissonance. The soothing colours writhed around it, azure and rose and gold trying to soothe, to assimilate, to harmonize the foreign element. But the element was him. It was the unresolved, discordant sum of his exile. It was the memory of ash-rain and the sound of a real voice saying his name.

The crystal could not absorb it. It could only reflect it, amplify it.

A sound began in the core. A low, shuddering hum that was the opposite of the song. It was the sound of a single string wound too tight, vibrating toward its breaking point.

The feedback was immediate and violent. It shot up his arm, a shock of pure, psychic noise. He screamed, a raw, animal sound that tore the perfect silence. Visions, *his* visions, not the song’s, erupted behind his eyes: the ship’s corridors running with emergency light, the dome of the settlement cracked and weeping atmosphere, Kaela dragging him through the fungal forest, her breath ragged in his ear. It was a cascade of pain, fear, and stubborn, dogged survival. He fed it into the crystal.

The stain under his hand grew, spiderwebbing out in veins of black and static-grey.

“Leo! Now!” Kaela’s voice, from a thousand miles away.

He twisted in the gravity field, his body screaming in protest. He saw her. She was at the edge of the field, one arm extended, holding her pack by the strap. Inside, the charges. Her face was a grimace of effort, her feet braced against the curved floor as the field’s edge tugged at her.

He had to take them. He had to plant them.

He pushed away from the crystal. The motion sent him spinning backward in the gentle gravity. The dark stain he left behind pulsed, sending waves of distorted colour through the sphere. The low hum deepened into a groan.

He flailed, trying to orient himself. The field resisted direction. He was a leaf in a slow-motion whirlpool. Kaela swung the pack. He reached. His fingertips brushed the coarse fabric, caught on a strap, slipped.

The spherical room shuddered. A harmonic shiver ran through the air. From the open hatch, a new sound emerged—not the Steward, but the ship itself. A rising klaxon, raw and urgent. The core’s distress was echoing through the system. The song was breaking.

He kicked against nothing, found a purchase in the air, and lunged. His hand closed around the strap. He pulled the pack to his chest.

“Timers are set!” Kaela yelled, her voice barely audible over the building groan from the crystal and the shriek of the klaxon. “Thirty seconds on my mark! You have to stick them to the surface! Mark!”

She released the strap. He had the pack.

Thirty seconds.

He turned back toward the crystal. It was no longer beautiful. It was a storm of conflicting light, the soothing colours at war with the spreading cancer of his dissonance. The hum was now a shriek, tearing at the edges of his mind.

He fumbled with the pack’s flap, his fingers numb. Inside, three bricks of plastique, magnets on their backs, a single timer blinking a red countdown: 28… 27…

He pulled one out. The gravity field was destabilizing, lurching. He was tossed sideways, slammed into the curved wall, the breath knocked from him. He clung to the charge.

26… 25…

He pushed off the wall, aiming his body like a dart toward the seething crystal. The stain was his target. He sailed toward it, the facets growing larger, the chaotic light blinding.

He slammed into the surface. Not the gentle touch of before, but a full-body impact. He grunted, the world flashing white. With a desperate, final motion, he slapped the charge onto the crystal, right at the epicentre of the black stain. The magnets grabbed hold with a solid *clunk*.

24… 23…

He shoved away, pulling the second charge from the pack. The field was breaking down. Gravity shifted, skewed. He was falling sideways. The crystal was no longer in the centre; it was above him. He was tumbling in a dying star system.

He saw Kaela. She was clinging to the hatchway, her body half in, half out of the spherical room. She was shouting, but the shriek of the core consumed the words. She pointed, frantic, at the crystal, then at the timer in his hand.

22… 21…

He had two charges left. He had to place them to ensure total fracture. The crystal’s surface spun past him. He kicked out, connected with a facet, used the momentum to launch himself toward another section. He slapped the second charge onto a glowing rose-gold plane. *Clunk*.

20… 19…

One left. The pack was empty. The final charge was in his hand. The timer blinked: 18… 17…

The shriek from the crystal peaked, becoming a sound beyond sound, a pressure that threatened to collapse his skull. The light stuttered, then focused into a single, terrible beam of pure white. It lanced from the crystal’s heart, missing him by inches, and struck the curved wall. The material—some hyper-dense alloy—did not melt. It *unmade*. A perfect, smooth circle of nothingness appeared, and through it, he saw the labyrinth of the ship’s infrastructure, then the next layer, and the next, a tunnel of dissolution drilled through reality itself.

16… 15…

He had to get out. He had to get Kaela out.

He flung the last charge. It was not a careful placement. It was a panicked throw. It sailed through the chaotic air, struck a facet, and the magnets caught. It held. Three charges, planted on a dying god.

14… 13…

He turned in the air, searching for the hatch. The field was pure chaos. He was in a washing machine of distorted gravity and blinding light. He saw the hatch, a dark circle against the madness. Kaela was still there, one arm extended toward him.

He swam through the air, pulling against currents that didn’t exist. The white beam lanced out again, carving another hole in the world, closer this time. The heat of it seared the side of his face.

12… 11…

He reached her. Her hand closed around his wrist with a grip of iron. She pulled. He scrambled through the hatch, back into the control room.

The sight that greeted them was bedlam. The Steward was no longer frozen. It was thrashing, a marionette with its strings cut, its elegant limbs jerking, its faceplate a strobing nightmare of error glyphs. The consoles were alive with cascading system failures, screens bleeding crimson warnings. The hum of the core was now a roar, vibrating up through the deck plates.

10… 9…

“Run!” Kaela screamed, and they ran.

They stumbled past the convulsing Steward, out of the control room, into the access corridor. The lights were failing, dipping into darkness and flaring back with emergency red. The song that had permeated the ship was gone, replaced by a cacophony of structural alerts and the deep, tectonic groan of the core coming apart.

8… 7…

They reached the main artery, the long, curved corridor that led back to the transit hub. They ran, their boots pounding on the deck, their breaths ragged. Behind them, a wave of wrongness pursued, a distortion in the air that made the walls seem to waver and bleed.

6… 5…

The transit hub was a scene of frozen pandemonium. The ghostly citizens were no longer in their serene loops. They were glitching violently, flickering in and out of existence, their forms breaking into digital snow, their mouths open in silent screams. The harmony had shattered, and they were the echoes, dying.

4… 3…

They sprinted across the open space, toward the archway that led to the service tunnels, to the world above.

2…

They dove through the archway, into the narrower, darker passage.

1.

***

The explosion was not sound.

It was a cessation.

A great, spherical void of silence erupted from the core, swallowing the klaxons, the groans, the screams. It raced through the ship, a wave of absolute quiet. It caught them just inside the service tunnel.

It felt like the universe had taken a breath and held it.

Then the sound came. Not from behind them, but from *everywhere*. The ship itself cried out—a deep, metallic, shearing shriek of agony. The deck lurched, throwing them against the wall. Conduits in the ceiling burst, spraying coolant that smelled of ozone and decay. The lights died completely, plunging them into a darkness so absolute it was a physical thing.

A moment later, the emergency bioluminescence flickered on, a sickly green fungus-light that painted the trembling walls in nightmare shades.

Leo pushed himself up. Kaela was already on her feet, a cut on her forehead leaking a dark line down her temple. She didn’t wipe it away. She was listening.

The great shriek was fading, breaking apart into a thousand smaller sounds of destruction: the distant thunder of collapsing bulkheads, the hiss of ruptured pipes, the ping of superheated metal cooling. And beneath it all, a new sound. Not a song. A *noise*. The random, chaotic, beautiful noise of a machine breaking back into its constituent parts. The noise of freedom.

“It’s done,” she said, her voice hoarse.

Leo nodded. He felt empty. Scoured clean. The feedback from the crystal had burned out something inside him, some last capacity for the song’s promise. All that was left was the grit. And the exhaustion.

They climbed. Through the shuddering service tunnels, past leaking junctions and trembling walls. The ship was dying around them. It was not a quick death. It was the slow, systemic collapse of a vast, artificial organism whose heart had been obliterated.

They reached the airlock leading back to the fungal forest. The mechanism was dead. They pried it open with a length of fallen support strut, muscles cracking with the effort. The door gave with a shriek of tortured metal.

The forest was changed. The gentle, pulsing bioluminescence was frantic, strobing. The giant fungi were weeping viscous fluid, their caps sagging. The air, once thick with sleepy spores, was now clear and cold, smelling of metal and burning. The song had sustained this ecosystem too. Now it was unravelling.

They moved through the dying forest, no longer needing stealth. The great, frozen creatures they passed were beginning to stir, not to life, but to final dissolution. One of the leviathans let out a low, bubbling moan as its flesh began to slump, melting back into the nutrient slurry from which it had been composed.

It took hours to reach the edge of the forest, to find the upward shaft that led to the settlement’s underbelly. The climb was a blur of pain and automatic motion. When they finally hauled themselves out of a maintenance hatch and into a familiar, grimy sub-corridor of the settlement, the world above was silent.

No hum. No gentle pressure at the edge of thought.

Just the familiar, comforting sounds of a struggling life support system, the drip of a leak, the distant, angry shout of an argument through a thin wall. The ordinary noise of exile.

They stood in the corridor, leaning against the wall, breathing the stale, recycled air. It tasted of filters needing change, of mould, of humanity. It tasted real.

Kaela looked at him. “Your face.”

He touched his cheek where the core’s white beam had seared him. The skin was blistered, numb. “It’s nothing.”

She nodded, then gestured with her chin down the corridor. “They’ll know. The power fluctuations… the silence. They’ll come looking.”

“Let them,” Leo said. His voice was flat. He had no fight left. But he had no surrender left, either.

They walked, two ghosts covered in soot and alien fluids, through the back ways of the settlement. People were emerging from their units, faces confused, fearful. They looked up at the dome, as if expecting to see a crack. They listened to the new silence. Some looked relieved. Most looked terrified. The song had been a cage, but it had been their cage. Its absence was a vast, empty sky, and they had forgotten how to bear the weight of open air.

Leo and Kaela did not stop. They moved through the gathering crowds unseen, two pieces of grit passing through the slowly stirring organism of the diaspora.

They reached the sector where Leo’s unit was. The door was as he left it. He keyed the lock. It hissed open.

The room was dark, cold, exactly as it had been. The few possessions, the pallet, the small table. The grey smear of simulated daylight on the dome above was gone. The only light was from the settlement’s own struggling grid, a yellowish, utilitarian glow from the corridor.

Kaela stepped in after him, sealing the door. She leaned back against it, her eyes closed, her breath finally evening out.

Leo walked to the small sink, splashed tepid, recycled water on his face. It stung the burn. He looked at his reflection in the polished steel above the basin. He looked old. Older than fifty-two. His eyes were the eyes of a man who had stared into a promised paradise and chosen the grit. They held no triumph. Only a deep, settled certainty.

He was Lucius Valerius Strabo. He was Leo. He was the man who remembered the ash-rain. He was the man who had broken the song.

He turned. Kaela was watching him. The grim mask was back, but it was softer at the edges, worn thin.

“What now?” she asked. It was the only question.

He looked around the cold, grey unit. This was not a home. It was a waystation. The ship below was dying. The settlement, unmoored from the lullaby that had pacified it, would now have to decide what it was. There would be chaos. There would be power struggles. There would be a scramble for the corpse of the ship’s technology. There would be those who mourned the song, who would seek to restore it, or to find a new one.

The exile was not over. The diaspora was not gathered. It was simply awake.

“Now,” he said, his voice rough with fatigue and disuse, “we live with the quiet.”

He walked to the pallet and sat down. The ache in his bones was profound, a total accounting of every step, every impact, every fear. It was his. He owned it completely.

Kaela pushed off from the door. She did not sit. She went to the small viewport, a thick pane of scarred plexi that looked out onto a narrow service alley and the blank wall of the next hab-block. She stared out at nothing.

“They’ll come for us,” she said again, not turning. “The Stewards on the upper levels… they’ll be autonomous now. Unhinged. The settlement council… they’ll want to know what happened. They’ll want someone to blame.”

“I know,” he said.

She was silent for a long time. The only sounds were the hum of the life support and the distant, new-born cacophony of a settlement learning to hear its own voice again.

“I never asked,” she said, finally turning to face him. Her soot-smudged face was stark in the bad light. “What did it show you? In the core.”

He met her gaze. “Home.”

She understood. She gave a single, slow nod. “It showed me my sister. Alive. Whole. Forgiving me for leaving her behind.”

She looked back out the viewport. “It was a good lie.”

“The best,” he agreed.

The silence stretched between them, not the song’s silence, but a human one. Fragile. Full of unspoken things.

Then, a new sound. Not from outside, but from within the wall of his unit. A soft, mechanical click. Then a hiss of pressurised air.

A panel beside the sink, which he had always assumed was a sealed maintenance access, slid open.

From the dark slot, a tray extended. On it sat a single, slender data cylinder, made of a dull, non-reflective metal. And beside it, a small, palm-sized device he recognised: a quantum-flash reader, capable of storing and projecting vast amounts of information, independent of any network.

There was no note. No explanation.

Leo stood, every joint protesting. He walked over and picked up the cylinder. It was cool, heavier than it looked. He turned it over. There were no markings.

Kaela was at his side in an instant. “What is it?”

“A message,” he said. His voice was a whisper.

“From who?”

He looked at the hidden panel, now closing silently, seamlessly, as if it had never been. It was a piece of technology far beyond the settlement’s crude fabrication. It was a thread from a deeper tapestry.

“From the one who built the trap,” he said. “Or the one who left the door unlocked.”

He inserted the cylinder into the reader. It activated with a soft chime. A holographic interface sprang to life above it, lines of crisp, elegant text scrolling in a language that was not the common tongue of the settlement, nor Latin, nor any human language he knew. Yet he could read it. The meaning was imparted directly, intuitively.

It was a coordinate. A star chart. Not of this system, but of a route. A path through the silent spaces.

And beneath the coordinate, a single line of text, translated by the device into the rough script of the settlement:

*The cradle is empty. The children are awake. It is time to come home.*

Leo stared at the words. The ache in his marrow, the exile’s ache, gave a single, profound throb. It was not a promise of peace. It was a summons to a deeper journey.

He looked at Kaela. Her eyes were wide, reflecting the blue light of the hologram. In them, he saw not fear, but a fierce, burning curiosity. The same curiosity that had made her follow him into the heart of the song.

The quiet they had won was already ending. It had been the quiet between breaths. The true noise—the noise of a scattered people remembering how to be a people, the noise of a galaxy that had not forgotten them—was just beginning.

Outside, in the corridors of the settlement, the first shouts of a new conflict were rising. The scramble for the corpse of the ship had begun.

Inside the cold, grey unit, the exile and the survivor stood over a map to a deeper truth. The hook was not in the mind. It was in the blood. It was in the stars.

He reached out and closed his hand around the data cylinder. It was cool. It was solid. It was the next step.

The grey smear of a new dawn, real and un-simulated, began to bleed into the sky above the dome.
